<p>19.0 BIBLIOGRAPHY...120</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="course-overview">COURSE OVERVIEW<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#course-overview" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Dear Learner,</p>
<p>At various times in your life, you have been a member of some kind of organization like Nurses association, church group, sports team, or business group. The organizations differ from one another in many ways. Some, like the association may be organized more formally while others like a church group may be less formally organized. Regardless of how they differ, they have some basic things in common. One of the most obvious is that they have a goal or purpose. The organization develops a method of achieving the goals. The organization will have a leader or manager responsible for helping the organization to achieve the goals. Without a manager, the organization may not forge forward because it will be like a ship with no captain.</p>
<p>This course is specifically about how nurse managers can help their organizations set and achieve their goals.</p>
<p>The course introduces you to the concept of nursing management. As you are aware by now, the importance of management in health care organizations cannot be overemphasized. This is mainly because we are now focusing more on productivity and quality of services. The nurses are found at all levels of the health care organizations where they occupy positions of leadership and management. To lead and manage successfully, the nurse must possess the necessary knowledge, skills and attitudes. The aim of this course is therefore to equip you with these to enable you to function efficiently and effectively as a nurse manager.</p>
<p>The Nursing management course is divided into 10 topics each comprising of specific Lectures. The first topic discusses the concept of management and developments in management theory including an overview of selected theories of management. Topic 2 focuses on leadership theories and styles as well as principles of management and how a nurse manager applies them in practice. Topics 3 comprises of lectures focusing on some of the most important functions of nursing management which include planning, organizing, staffing, delegating and controlling.</p>
<p>Page 6 of 114</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The eighth and ninth Topics look at the management of resources including human, financials, materials and time and change management respectively. The final topic focuses on quality management in nursing care services.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="course-objectives">COURSE OBJECTIVES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#course-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-16eed4f3.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<p>By the end of this course, of this course, you should be able:</p>
<ol>
<li>Explain the concept and theories of management.</li>
<li>Describe the principles and functions of management.</li>
<li>Discuss various forms of resource management.</li>
<li>Explain ways of managing time and change.</li>
<li>Discuss the elements of health information systems.</li>
<li>Discuss quality management in nursing care services.</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-0-concept-and-overview-of-theories-of-management">1.0 CONCEPT AND OVERVIEW OF THEORIES OF MANAGEMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-0-concept-and-overview-of-theories-of-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lecture-1-definition-and-theories-of-management">LECTURE 1 - DEFINITION AND THEORIES OF MANAGEMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lecture-1-definition-and-theories-of-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-1-overview">1.1 OVERVIEW<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-overview" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>During the course of your practice as a nurse and in your day to day life, you have been involved in some form of management either as a team leader or a manager.</p>
<p>This section will introduce you to the concept of management and briefly describe the developments in some of the management theories which have influenced our practice.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-0daa2741.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-2-objectives">1.2 OBJECTIVES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>By the end of this lecture you should be able to:-</p>
<ul>
<li>Define the concept of management.</li>
<li>Explain various scientific management theories.</li>
<li>Describe how these management theories apply to nursing practice.</li>
</ul>
<p>During the course of your practice as a nurse and in your day to day life, you have come across some aspects of management.</p>
<p>This section will introduce you to the concept of management and briefly describe the developments in some of the management theories which have influenced our practice.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-a74fa52b.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg"></p>
<p>In your own terms, how do you define management?</p>
<p>Write down your answer.</p>
<p>You may find that the definition you have written is similar to the ones given by other authors. Different writers have defined management in various ways and there is no generally accepted definition. The most common ones are:-</p>
<ul>
<li>Getting things done through people.</li>
<li>The process of achieving organizational goals by working with and through people and resources.</li>
<li>The process of acquiring and combining human, financial, informational and physical resources to achieve the organizational goal of producing a service or product</li>
</ul>
<p>As you can see, the above definitions are based on two (2) main principles:</p>
<p>The first puts emphasis on people who are the most important resource to get work done. While the next focuses on the achievement of organizational goals and commitment to the goals as an important task of management.</p>
<p>Page 10 of 114</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-3-management-theories">1.3 MANAGEMENT THEORIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-3-management-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Just like in other areas of nursing, you have heard of theories and how we base our practice on them. Management theories also describe how managers conduct activities, and keep institutions operating in an effective way in order to meet the objectives.</p>
<p>The different approaches used by managers have gone through an evolutionary process. In practice none of them alone will be sufficient.</p>
<p>Most of the managers use a combination of the approaches to create effectiveness within the organization. The three main classifications include:-</p>
<ul>
<li>Classical Theories (Scientific Management)</li>
<li>Human Relations (Behavioral Approaches)</li>
<li>Systems and Contingency Approaches</li>
</ul>
<p>Let us now describe the above and how they influence management.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-3-1-classical-theories-scientific-management">1.3.1 Classical Theories (Scientific Management)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-3-1-classical-theories-scientific-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The scientific approach of management started in the later part of 19th and early 20th centuries. The theories were primarily concerned with structure and activities of formal or official organizations. They laid foundations of ideas about organization of people at work and organization of work.</p>
<p>The approach relied on systematic information collection, analysis and identification of causes and effects followed by effective organization of management structure. The objective was to develop basic principles that could guide the design, creation and maintenance of organizations. The emphasis was on efficiency and effectiveness. The following are some of the prominent figures who put forward the ideas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-henri-fayol-1841-1925">1. Henri Fayol (1841-1925)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-henri-fayol-1841-1925" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>He was a French industrialist and theorist who worked as a mining engineer, spent all his life in that one company, where he rose to the position of managing Director. The Company grew and prospered under his leadership. In 1916 he published major work on management.</p>
<p>Fayol outlined the following 6 key activities which a manager is involved in:</p>
<ul>
<li>Technical activities eg. Production.</li>
<li>Commercial activities eg. Buying and selling.</li>
<li>Security activities eg. Safeguarding property</li>
<li>Accounting activities eg. Providing financial information.</li>
</ul>
<p>As the manager carries out these activities, Fayol identified four main functions which enable her/him perform effectively and efficiently. Namely: planning, organizing, coordinating and controlling. These will be discussed in details under topic 4.</p>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Similarly, Fayol identified a set of 14 principles which managers might apply as they perform their duties. Some of the important ones include division of labour and specialization, chain of command, centralization and responsibility. These have been adopted by other followers of the classical school of thought and many have been absorbed into modern organizations. They are discussed in more details under Topi 2.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-frederic-w-taylor-1856-1915">2. Frederic W. Taylor: (1856-1915)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-frederic-w-taylor-1856-1915" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>He was an engineer who was instrumental in conducting research on methods of training workers for increased production. In his systematic analysis of work, he observed that few people put more than minimal effort and had a tendency to take things easy. He believed in the principle "best management is a true science". Like Fayol he was a practical manager theorist. He worked on problems of achieving greater efficiency. His idea of "Scientific" Management was born. Tylor was interested in efficiency of working methods. According to Taylor the objective of management is to secure the maximum prosperity for the employer, as well as each employee.</p>
<p>Taylor's system for work improvement consisted of the following steps:-</p>
<ul>
<li>Observing the workers performance through time and motion study. This was done to determine one best way to carry out each task.</li>
<li>Scientifically selecting the best worker to perform each job, that is, the person with characteristics and abilities needed to carry out job tasks in an efficient way.</li>
<li>Training the selected worker to perform tasks in most efficient manner.</li>
<li>Appointing a few highly skilled workers in managerial positions.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-e9eab66b.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<p>How are some of the views expressed by Fayol and Taylor applied in nursing situations?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-max-weber-1864-1920">3. Max Weber (1864-1920)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-max-weber-1864-1920" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>He lived around the same period of history as those early pioneers of management thought. He was an academician and a sociologist.</p>
<p>Weber's interest in organization was from the point of view of their structures. He wanted to find out why people in organizations obeyed those in authority over them. His observations and conclusions from studies were published and the term bureaucracy was used to describe a rational form of organization that exists today in every enterprise and business.</p>
<p>He identified three types of legitimate authority namely:</p>
<ul>
<li>Traditional â€“ where acceptance of those in authority arises from tradition and custom (eg. Monarchies, tribal hierarchies).</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Rational â€“ legal-acceptance arises out of the office or position of person in authority as bounded by the rules and procedures of the organization. This exists in many organizations and is the one Weber describes in terms of bureaucracy.</li>
<li>Legitimate Authority â€“ implies acceptance of rule by those over whom it is to be exercised. Power is unilateral â€“ enables one person to force another to behave in a certain way whether by means of strength or by rewards.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-main-features-of-bureaucracy">4. Main Features of Bureaucracy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-main-features-of-bureaucracy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The main features of bureaucracy focused on the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>A continuous organization of functions bound by rules. Every organization has rules, regulations and policies that govern the employees.</li>
<li>Specified spheres of competence eg. Specialization of work or degree of authority allocated and the rules governing the exercise of authority.</li>
<li>A hierarchical arrangement of offices (jobs). This is where one level of jobs is subject to control by the next higher level. Fayol referred to this principle as the chain of command.</li>
<li>Appointment to offices is made on grounds of technical competence.</li>
<li>Official position exists in their own right and job holder has no rights to a particular position.
Rules, decisions and actions are formulated and recorded in writing.</li>
</ul>
<p>Weber felt bureaucracy was capable of attaining highest degree of efficiency in organizations. Although Weber meant well, today bureaucracy has been misused to imply slowness, inefficiency and is seen as too rigid to respond to the current rapid changes in society.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-e32df3bc.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg"></p>
<p>Identify the similarities and differences in approaches to management. between the above theorists</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-benefits-arising-from-scientific-management">5. Benefits Arising From Scientific Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-benefits-arising-from-scientific-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are some benefits of the above approach. These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Rational approach to the organization of work enables tasks and processes to be measured with a considerable degree of accuracy.</li>
<li>Measurement of tasks and processes provide useful information on which to base improvements in working methods.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>By improving working methods, this increases productivity.</li>
<li>Enables employees to be paid by results (incentive payments).</li>
<li>Stimulates management into adapting a more positive role in leadership.</li>
<li>Provides the foundations which modern work study and other quantitative techniques could be soundly based.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-drawbacks-to-scientific-management">6. Drawbacks to Scientific Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-drawbacks-to-scientific-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following are some of the weaknesses of scientific theories:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reduces the workers role to that of a rigid adherence to methods and procedures over which he had no discretion.</li>
<li>Leads to fragmentation of work on account of its emphasis on the analysis and organization of individual tasks or operations.</li>
<li>It generates a â€œcarrot and stickâ€ approach to the motivation of employees by enabling pay to be compared to output.</li>
<li>Puts the planning and control of workplace activities exclusively in the hands of the management.</li>
<li>Rules out any unrealistic bargaining about wages since every job is measured and rated scientifically.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lecture-2">LECTURE 2:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lecture-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-3-2-behavioral-approach-theories">1.3.2 Behavioral Approach Theories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-3-2-behavioral-approach-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>In the previous lecture we covered the concept of management and scientific theories of management as put forward by some early theorists. During this lecture, we are going to discuss behavioral theories. This is the second set of classification of management theories which emerged as a reaction to Scientific Management</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-ae1027a2.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">OBJECTIVES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this lecture, you should be able to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Describe behavioral theories</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Differentiate between the different approaches to behavioral theories</li>
<li>Discuss the implications of these theories to nursing practice.</li>
</ol>
<p>Behavioral theories are also referred to as Human Relations or Psychological Theories.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-c2238a29.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reflect-what-did-you-learn-about-scientific-management">Reflect: What did you learn about Scientific management?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reflect-what-did-you-learn-about-scientific-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>According to behavioral approach theories, the fundamental idea to management is that people's needs are the decisive factor in achieving organization effectiveness and that human factor determines human motivation, group relations and leadership.</p>
<p>These differ from the scientific in that the latter focused on use of people as a support service to machines (and that economic forces are more or less motivators of human performance). The behavior approach theories focus on the manner in which managers interact with subordinates. Managers must know why subordinates behave as they do. The theories are based on analysis of the relationship between human factors and productivity. Besides meeting the economic goals of the organization, the managers must try to improve the physiological, social and psychological needs of workers which in turn improve productivity.</p>
<p>The following is a brief discussion of some of these theories which may apply in our situations within our institutions:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-elton-mayo">1. Elton Mayo<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-elton-mayo" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The Human Relations theories began through work of Elton Mayo. He is described as founder of Human relations movement.</p>
<p>Mayo was a psychologist, who with others conducted a series of experiments at the Hawthorne plant of general Electrical Company in Chicago â€“ referred to as Hawthorne Experiments (1927-1932). He focused on the workers and not work. He studied people in terms of social relationships at work. He evaluated the effects of changes in physical working conditions which according to scientific management should cause significant variations in productivity.</p>
<p>Mayo adjusted physical working conditions like lighting, noise levels and noted the resulting output changes among the workers. Contrary to expectations, the output increased whenever conditions were changed even if they were made worse. The workers tended to interact more and formed informal groupings. The conclusion was that man was a social animal and individuals cannot work in isolation. Thus, at work and outside group membership is important to individuals and leads to establishment of informal groups within the official formal groupings.</p>
<p>Page 15 of 114</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The findings concluded that group's relationships were found to be far more important in determining employee behavior than were physical conditions and working practices imposed by management.</p>
<p>In addition to the above findings, the Research also established the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Employee behavior depends primarily on the social and organizational circumstances of work.</li>
<li>Leadership style, group cohesion and job satisfaction are major determinants of the outputs of working groups.</li>
<li>Employees work better if they are given a wide range of tasks to complete â€“ Application of division of labor can make work boring and meaningless, and thus decrease productivity.</li>
<li>Supervisors and managers need to be aware of these social needs and cater for them if workers are to collaborate with the official organization rather than work against it.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-abraham-maslow-s-theory-of-hierarchy-of-needs">2. Abraham Maslow's Theory of Hierarchy of Needs:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-abraham-maslow-s-theory-of-hierarchy-of-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In your basic training you may have learned about Maslow's human basic needs in relation to patient's needs and priority setting.</p>
<p>Maslow, who was a psychologist, made considerable influence on development in Management Theory in 50's and 60's.</p>
<p>According to Maslow's, every human being has basic needs and people are motivated by the desire to satisfy these needs. As one need is satisfied another appears to take its place and the individual is then motivated to satisfy the new need. Maslow developed the hierarchy of needs with the physiologic needs at the bottom, which the individuals must satisfy before realizing higher level psychological needs. People tend to satisfy their needs systematically until a particular group of needs is satisfied and a person's behavior will be dominated by them e.g a hungry person will not be motivated by safety shelter, until hunger is satisfied.</p>
<p>Maslow visualized needs as arranged in hierarchical order starting with basic and going to higher ones depending on individuals as shown in Figure 1 below.</p>
<p>Physiological - need for survival which includes food, oxygen and sleep, sex etc.</p>
<p>Safety needs - need for security eg. Job at work place, shelter and stable environment.</p>
<p>Love and belonging - need for affectionate relations with others or to a belong to a group.</p>
<p>Self esteem - need for self respect and recognition. At this stage people strive for power, achievement and status.</p>
<p>Self-actualization - need for self fulfillment or to grow to ones fullest potential. This is a culmination of all other</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-5dc07d6e.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg">
FIGURE 1: Maslow's Hierarchy of Human Basic NeedS</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-10aae982.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg"></p>
<p>What are some implications of Maslow's Hierarchy of Human Basic Needs in nursing management?</p>
<ul>
<li>Implications of Maslow's Theory to Practice:
The nurse manager should realize that people at work experience a variety of needs.
Therefore the Managers/supervisors should identify ways in which to meet the people's/individual needs to motivate them to work. It is the responsibility of the manager to fulfill the needs of employees.
One of the criticisms of this theory is that systematic movement up the hierarchy may not always be consistent.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Douglas McGregor's - Theory X and Theory Y</li>
</ol>
<p>In his contribution to management behavioral theories, McGregor came up with certain assumptions about behavior of employees. He identified these as Theory X and Y. McGregor noted that managers make these assumptions about their subordinates or employees as they supervise them.</p>
<p>i. Theory X</p>
<p>In this first set of assumptions termed as "theory X," the employee is seen as:</p>
<ul>
<li>An average human being who has with an inherent dislike for work and will avoid it if possible.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>As a result of the above most people must be coerced, controlled, directed and threatened with punishment in order to produce.</li>
<li>The average human being has to be closely directed and wishes to avoid responsibility and only wants security.</li>
</ul>
<p>ii. Theory Y</p>
<p>The second set of assumptions "theory Y" regard people in a more favorable way.</p>
<ul>
<li>Employees are seen, as liking work, which is as natural as rest or play.</li>
<li>Human beings do not have to be controlled or coerced as long as commitment to the organization is present.</li>
<li>Under proper conditions, people will not only accept but also seek responsibility.</li>
</ul>
<p>Theory X and Y made impact in Managerial world because they helped to identify forms of management style.</p>
<ul>
<li>Implications to Practice</li>
</ul>
<p>Theory "X" and theory Y" are concerned with the attitudes management has towards employee. The manager holding theory X assumptions tends to be tough, authoritarian and supporting tight controls with punishments. As a result he tends to supervise the workers very closely.</p>
<ul>
<li>On the other hand the manager using theory Y believes in self control, is democratic, and consults staff.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-1151561a.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<p>Which aspects of this theory do you think are better for a Nurse Manager in his/her day to day work?</p>
<p>NB: A blend between "Theory X" and "Theory Y" is more likely to provide effective management.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Fredrick Herzberg's â€“ Two Factor Theory (Motivation â€“ Hygiene Theory)</li>
</ol>
<p>Herzberg is another theorist who made a lot of contribution to behavioral approaches. His work which was done in 1959 on motivation looked at the employees and the factors which contributed to job satisfaction or dissatisfaction. He came up with the conclusion that the factors, which gave rise to satisfaction, were called "motivators". Those, which contributed towards dissatisfaction, were called "Hygiene factors". Motivators can bring positive satisfaction, hygiene factors can only serve to prevent dissatisfaction.</p>
<p>i. Motivational Factors</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>According to Herzberg, these are job conditions that, if present, tend to influence high levels of motivation and job satisfaction. He described the motivational factors or satisfiers as some of the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Achievements</li>
<li>Recognition</li>
<li>Advancements â€“ possibility of growth</li>
<li>The work itself</li>
<li>Responsibility</li>
</ul>
<p>ii. Hygiene factors (Dissatisfies)</p>
<p>These were noted as those factors, which are more related to the environment of the work. The most important factors were:</p>
<ul>
<li>Company policy and administration</li>
<li>Supervision- technical aspect</li>
<li>Salary</li>
<li>Interpersonal relations</li>
<li>Working conditions</li>
</ul>
<p>When these were in line with employee requirements, they would smooth the path of a working life, but when out of line with employees' expectations, they could be a source of difficulty and complaints and provide grounds for dissatisfaction.</p>
<p>Therefore, if motivators are absent from the job, the employee will experience dissatisfaction. Even if hygiene factors are provided for, they will not in themselves bring about substantial job satisfaction.</p>
<ul>
<li>Implications of Two Factor Theory to Practice</li>
</ul>
<p>The nurse managers need to use both Hygiene and Motivator factors to recruit and retain staff. Hygiene factors do not create job satisfaction but they must be in place for work to get done. If not, the factors will dissatisfy staff.</p>
<p>Work performance can be improved by using motivator factors.</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Expectancy Theory</li>
</ol>
<p>This is based on the work of V.H Vroom who was an American who came up with the theory in the 60's.</p>
<p>It was observed that previous motivation theories were based on content of motivation but Vroom based his theory on process.</p>
<p>The Key point is that an individual's behavior is formed NOT on objective reality but on his subjective perception of that reality. The core of theory relates to how a person perceives the relationship between three things: Effort, performance and rewards. It focused mainly on factors involved in stimulating an individual to put effort into something since it is the basis of motivation.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>As illustrated in Fig.2, Vroom concluded that there are 3 factors each based on the individual's personal perception of the situation. These are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Expectancy â€“ This refers to the extent to which individual's perception or belief of individual's particular act will produce a particular outcome.</li>
<li>Instrumentality - This is the extent to which the individual perceives that effective performance will lead to desired rewards.</li>
<li>Valence - This is the strength of belief that attractive rewards are potentially available.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-abe5ff2c.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg">
FIG. 2. Expectancy Theory</p>
<p>Vroom observed that effort alone may not necessarily lead to effective performance. There are other factors involved like individual characteristics like personality, knowledge and skills. Also the way, in which a person perceives his role e.g for prospect of promotion, there will be attractive prospect or (valence).</p>
<p>Rewards are either Intrinsic or extrinsic. Intrinsic rewards are gained from fulfilling higher level personal needs e.g self esteem and personal growth while Extrinsic are provided by organization and outside control of an individual e.g. pay, promotion, working conditions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="implications-of-expectancy-theory">- Implications of Expectancy Theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#implications-of-expectancy-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The theory therefore takes a comprehensive view of the motivational process.</p>
<p>It indicates that individuals will only act when they have a reasonable expectancy that their behavior will lead to the desired outcomes.</p>
<p>The theory also stresses the importance of individual perceptions of reality in the motivational process and implies that job satisfaction follows effective job performance rather than the other way round.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>As a result, it has led to development in work redesign where emphasis has been laid on intrinsic job factors.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-452318ad.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="compare-and-contrast-between-herzberg-s-and-expectancy-theories">Compare and contrast between Herzberg's and Expectancy theories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#compare-and-contrast-between-herzberg-s-and-expectancy-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="c393a7d4">6. Theory Z â€“ The Japanese Approach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c393a7d4" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The theory describes an approach to employee motivation based on management practices of Japanese. The phrase was coined by the American exponent to these approaches, W. Ouchi (1981), as he attempted to adapt Japanese practices to western firms. Attention has been given to success of Japanese manufacturing industries and one of the key successes is their approach to their management of resources, especially people.</p>
<p>According to Ouchi, the following are features of the theory which are personnel related factors;-</p>
<ul>
<li>There is a high degree of mutual trust and loyalty between management and employees.</li>
<li>Career paths are non-specialized with life â€“long job rotation as a central feature of career development.</li>
<li>Performance appraisal is long-term (First appraisal takes place 10 years after joining the Company).</li>
<li>Decision making is shared at all levels.</li>
<li>The approach has a strong sense of collective responsibility for the success of the organization and cooperative effort.</li>
</ul>
<p>The critics of the theory have identified some weaknesses in this approach. Firstly, there is a slow process of decision-making. People also do not want to take any risks. There is an element of imprisoning effect of lifetime employment in one Company. Lastly there exists docile nature of trade unions where their impact is not felt.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-3-3-the-systems-approach-to-management-theory">1.3.3 The Systems Approach to Management Theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-3-3-the-systems-approach-to-management-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-systems-theory">1. Systems Theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-systems-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is a more recent approach which views the organizations as a system of interrelated sets of activities which enable inputs to be converted into outputs. It studies key elements of organization in terms of their interaction with one another and with their external environment.</p>
<p>The theory seeks to explain or predict organization behavior in a multidimensional way by studying people, structure technology and environment at one and the same time.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>A system is defined as a collection of interrelated parts which form some whole. A system may be open or closed. Open systems interact with environment and social systems e.g. Organizations are open systems. Closed systems do not interact with their environment. The organization is therefore defined as a system of consciously coordinated personnel activities or forces (Basawanthappa,2007).</p>
<p>The systems theory explains that the organizations come into existence when there are persons able to communicate with each other and will contribute action to accomplish a purpose.</p>
<p>There are three major characteristics of open systems as shown in Fig.3</p>
<ul>
<li>Receive inputs or energy from environment.</li>
<li>Convert these inputs into outputs.</li>
<li>Discharge their outputs into environment.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-3eb33889.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg">
FIG 3. Open System</p>
<p>In relation to organizations the inputs include â€“ people, materials, information and finances. These are organized and activated so as to convert human skills and raw materials into products, services and other outputs which are discharged into environment.</p>
<p>The key feature is the interdependence on the environment which may be relatively stable or relatively uncertain at a particular point in time. This is important because organizations need to adapt to the changing environment.</p>
<p>The key variables to those adopting a systems approach to organizations are:</p>
<ul>
<li>People as individual and in groups.</li>
<li>Technology - technical requirements of work.
Organization structures</li>
<li>Environment - external conditions affecting the organization.</li>
</ul>
<p>The Systems theories study the relationship between these variables. The managers who use the Systems Theory study the relationship between these variables. Fig.4</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-d1df7b26.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-1c49106e.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p>Do you consider your organization an open or closed system? Write down the reasons for your answer(s)</p>
<ul>
<li>Advantages of systems approach</li>
</ul>
<p>The following are two advantages of systems approach:</p>
<ol>
<li>It enables managerial consequences of changes in environmental conditions to be analyzed methodically.</li>
<li>It looks at the organization as a whole considering all aspects of its relations.</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Drawbacks of systems approach</li>
</ul>
<p>Page 23 of 114</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Some of the weaknesses of this approach include:</p>
<ol>
<li>The abstract nature of systems theory makes it difficult to apply in practice.</li>
<li>Relationships and environments can be extremely complex and sometimes difficult to analyze systematically.</li>
<li>Theory is descriptive rather than prescriptive. It explains how organizations work together but cannot explain causes of motivation at work or why individuals perceive each other and organization in certain ways.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-contingency-approaches-to-management">3. Contingency Approaches to Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-contingency-approaches-to-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It has been observed that there is no clear cut distinction between the systems and contingency approaches.</p>
<p>A contingency approach builds on the diagnostic qualities of the systems approach in order to determine the most appropriate organizational design and management style for a given set of circumstances (Bennett, 1997).</p>
<p>In this approach, effective management is that which quickly identifies the key factors underlying a particular situation and hence applies measures specifically relevant to the case in question. The manager chooses the best combination in the light of prevailing conditions of the variables including; External environment, Technology factors and Human skills and motivation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="implications-of-contingency-theory">Implications of contingency theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#implications-of-contingency-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>In terms of nursing management, some of the implications of this approach are:</p>
<p>i. Managers should be flexible when designing or choosing a management style.
ii. Certain situations require strict supervision and an authoritarian approach while others call for relaxed and democratic manner.
iii. A Management style suitable for one set of circumstances need not be appropriate elsewhere.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="advantages-of-contingency-approach">Advantages of contingency approach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages-of-contingency-approach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The advantages of this approach are:</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i. Contingency approach does not seek to produce universal prescriptions (unlike Classical and Human Relations) approach to management.
ii. It is a situational approach Management which deals with relativities not absolutes.
iii. The approach adapts other approaches as part of a "mix" â€“ to be applied to an organization in particular circumstances.</p>
<ul>
<li>Weaknesses of contingency approach</li>
</ul>
<p>A number of the weaknesses have been identified. One is lack of unifying general principles might lead to inconsistency when taking decisions. The other is that individual Managers may not like altering their approaches and modes of behavior as circumstances change. There is huge variety of variables which potentially affect each management situation.</p>
<p>As a result, extensive training and experience are needed for successful application of contingency approach.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-1295e190.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<p>Summary</p>
<p>In this topic we have defined the concept of management which focuses on getting things done through people in order to achieve organizational goals. We also looked at how management has evolved over time through use of different approaches. These approaches were based on the theories of management which are classified as: Classical Theories (scientific management); Behavioral or Human Relations; Systems and Contingency approaches</p>
<p>From a practical point of view, the manager uses the approach which is most appropriate for a specific situation. In most cases, we usually use a mixture of all or some of them.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-99a88a4e.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg"></p>

<p>TOPIC 2:</p>
<p>2.0 LEADERSHIP IN NURSING</p>
<p>LECTURE 3: LEADERSHIP</p>
<p>As a practicing nurse you will spend most of your life working in health care institutions at all levels from junior nursing officer to the highest positions of responsibility. Whatever the role, all nurses lead and manage to some degree. This topic discusses leadership in nursing. You will be able to apply the theories of management, covered in the preceding chapter, as you carry out leadership responsibilities.</p>

<p>Page 27 of 114</p>
<ul>
<li>OBJECTIVES</li>
</ul>
<p>By the end of this topic, you should be able to:-</p>
<ol>
<li>Define leadership.</li>
<li>Describe the different styles of leadership.</li>
<li>Discuss how you can apply the styles of leadership in nursing practice</li>

<p>2.1 Leadership</p>
<p>The word leadership is an intriguing one and brings up images of all kinds of people. Some names that come to mind include; former leaders like Presidents Julius Nyerere, President Jomo Kenyatta and Nelson Mandela. In nursing, Florence Nightingale may be considered an example of a leader in the profession. You may also think of a relative or a nurse who had a noticeable effect on one's life and may be considered a leader.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-0f084718.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<p>Think of the best nurse leader you have ever known personally. Why do you think that person is/was a good leader? What makes her/him an effective leader?</p>
<p>-Who is a Leader?</p>
<p>A leader can be defined as anyone who uses interpersonal skills to influence others to accomplish a specific goal OR A person who exerts influence by using certain personal behaviours and strategies.</p>
<ul>
<li>Definition of Leadership</li>
</ul>
<p>Various people have defined leadership in various ways but all the definitions have a common theme which brings out the idea of influencing others to achieve a goal.</p>
<p>Douglas (1996), defines leadership as the use of one's skills to influence others to perform the best of their ability toward achievement of goals. It is the art of getting others to want to do what one deems important.</p>
<p>Leadership is not a fixed position; it is a process which changes as situations change. A manager leads by personally and actively working with the subordinates for two main reasons:</p>
<ol>
<li>To guide and motivate their behaviour to fit the plans that have been established.</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>To understand the feelings of employees and the problems they face as they translate the plans into actions.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-1-1-types-of-leadership">2.1.1 Types of Leadership<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-1-1-types-of-leadership" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Let us now look at the types of leadership. There are two types of leadership. The first is formal leadership where an appointed leader is chosen by administration and given official or legitimate authority. This has the greatest impact when followers accept the leader.</p>
<p>The second is Informal leadership. This type does not have official authority to direct activities of others. A leader is chosen by a group itself eg. social group, church organization or work group. One may become a leader because the members of the group respect her /him due to age, seniority or special competencies or personality.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-leaders">Types of Leaders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-leaders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Different types of leaders have been identified. The following are the most important ones:</p>
<ul>
<li>Charismatic Leader- This is the one who gains influence mainly from strength of personality and has exceptional qualities required to transform all those around into willing followers.</li>
<li>Traditional leader- A leader whose position is assured by birth e.g. kings or tribal chieftains. In this one few people can aspire to be leaders</li>
<li>Appointed leader â€“ This is the one whose influence arises directly out of his position, e.g. a manager or supervisor</li>
<li>Situational leader â€“ A leader whose influence is effective by being in the right place at the right time. The leader is looked at as someone who can assume leadership of a temporary measure over a specific period of time</li>
<li>Functional leader â€“ This is a leader who gets into a leadership position because of what he or she does rather than what they are.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-dd7426ec.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="give-examples-of-the-types-of-leaders-you-have-in-your-workplace-and-why-you-consider-them-so">Give examples of the types of leaders you have in your Workplace and why you consider them so.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#give-examples-of-the-types-of-leaders-you-have-in-your-workplace-and-why-you-consider-them-so" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-1-2-theories-of-leadership">2.1.2 Theories of Leadership<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-1-2-theories-of-leadership" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Leadership is a concept which has been with us since time immemorial. Various writers have attempted to explain leaders and leadership and some theories have been put forward.</p>
<p>We will look at the following two (2) theories:-</p>
<ol>
<li>Trait Theory of Leadership</li>
<li>Situational / Contingency Theory of Leadership</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-trait-theory-of-leadership">1. Trait theory of leadership<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-trait-theory-of-leadership" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the first theory that was first studied from 1900 - 1950. The theory says that leaders have certain sets of physical and emotional characteristics that are important for inspiring others towards a common goal. Some theorists who subscribe to this theory believe that</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>leaders are born with certain qualities that determine leadership ability and success. The leader is seen as gifted or develops certain characteristics eg. Physical (weight, height); Intellectual (knowledge, judgement); Personality (aggressiveness, dominance, authoritarian etc).</p>
<p>According to the theory the leader behaves according to the role expectations of the group.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-situational-contingency-theory-of-leadership">2. Situational / contingency theory of leadership<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-situational-contingency-theory-of-leadership" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this theory, leadership is seen more as a question of appropriate behavior than of personality or being in the right place at the right time.</p>
<p>The theory believes that leadership effectiveness depends on the relationship among the firstly, the leader's task at hand in terms of what requires to be accomplished. Secondly the interpersonal skills. This relates to how well the leader interacts with others. Finally the favorableness of the work situation.</p>
<p>However, there are critical factors which must be considered in the above relationship. These factors which will dictate the effectiveness of the leader include:</p>
<ol>
<li>The degree of trust and respect between the leader and follower.</li>
<li>The clarity of goals to be accomplished.</li>
<li>The ability of the leader to reward the followers and exert influence.</li>
</ol>
<p>As a result of all the above, leaders are viewed as able to adapt their style according to the present situation. For example, if one is faced with a situation, he/she considers its challenges and encourages an adaptive leadership style to understand the issue being faced.</p>
<p>The implication here is that, you as a nurse manager must assess each situation and determine appropriate action, based on the people involved.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-1-3-leadership-styles">2.1.3 Leadership Styles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-1-3-leadership-styles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The styles of leadership used by managers vary from organization to organization and are tailored to fit their needs and individual behaviour. There are too many kinds of leaders, personnel, tasks, organizations and environments for any one leadership style to apply.</p>
<p>Basically, there are three (3) styles of leadership namely; authoritarian (autocratic), democratic (participative) and laissez faire (permissive).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-authoritarian-autocratic">1. Authoritarian (autocratic)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-authoritarian-autocratic" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the style where the leader assumes full responsibility for all actions. The autocratic leader behaves in the following manner:</p>
<ul>
<li>Gives direct, clear and exact orders to his employees with detailed instructions as to what is to be done and how, leaving no or little room for employee initiative.</li>
<li>Delegates as little as possible and believes that he can do a better job than his followers. Has little or no confidence in workers.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Functions with high concern for task accomplishment but low concern for the people who perform these tasks.</li>
<li>Frequently exercises power sometimes with coercion and is dominating and insistent.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="implications">- Implications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#implications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For the manager who uses this style of leadership, there are implications to the employees. Such leadership may cause employees to lose interest and initiative, stop thinking for themselves because there is no need for independent thought or lose motivation. In addition, employees may find it necessary to adopt an attitude of obedience to the autocratic boss.</p>
<p>Due to lack of motivation, the employees may reluctantly give only minimum performance. As a result, less than optimal goals are achieved because the resources utilized are those of the manager only.</p>
<p>Some form of authoritarian leadership has been used in nursing - it is particularly suitable in situations of crisis or emergency when clear directions are of highest priority.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-democratic-leadership-participative-or-consultative">2. Democratic leadership (participative or consultative)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-democratic-leadership-participative-or-consultative" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As mentioned earlier, there is no one best leadership style. The next one is democratic leadership in which people are free to express themselves. This style is characterized by the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>It is "People oriented" and focuses attention on human aspects and on building effective work groups.</li>
<li>It has a collaborative spirit or joint effort which allows for group participation in decision making.</li>
<li>The leader assumes that employees are eager to perform their jobs and capable of doing so.</li>
<li>The group is seen as having responsibility for goal determination and achievement.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="implications">- Implications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#implications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Democratic leadership encourages enthusiasm, high morale and increased satisfaction among the employees.</p>
<p>Research has shown that this style of leadership leads to high productivity and is the most desirable form of management in a wide variety of work situations.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-laissez-faire-free-rein-permissive">3. Laissez-faire (free rein, permissive)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-laissez-faire-free-rein-permissive" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The other style of leadership is Laissez-faire which is at the opposite end of the continuum from authoritarian. In this style the leader lets people do what they want. The manager who</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>practices this style of leadership plays down his role in the group's activity and exercises minimum direction or control.</p>
<p>Similarly, the manager wants everyone to feel good about what they are doing.</p>
<p>He/she also avoids responsibility by relinquishing power to followers and permits them to engage in managerial activities eg. Decision making, planning, setting goals, structuring and controlling the organization.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="implications">- Implications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#implications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The Laissez-Faire style of leadership exists when a manager is too weak or too threatened to exercise the function of leadership or the manager has great need of approval and does not want to offend anybody. In this case the following can be observed:</p>
<ul>
<li>Little or no visible leadership.</li>
<li>Manager tends to be preoccupied with his own work.</li>
<li>No communication and directions to employee.</li>
<li>Very little teamwork - employees do as they wish.</li>
</ul>
<p>The style can be effective in highly motivated professional groups such as in research projects in which independent thinking is rewarded.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-6376da6d.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg"></p>
<p>Assume you are a ward/department manager of a Unit. State what your approach would be if you were</p>
<ul>
<li>an authoritarian manager</li>
<li>a democratic manager</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="qualities-of-effective-leadership">Qualities of effective leadership<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#qualities-of-effective-leadership" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You are familiar with the many qualities of a good nurse. For a leader to be effective in influencing others to perform, he/she requires to possess certain qualities. Since there are also many qualities, only a few will be mentioned. These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Empathy: Ability to look at things from another person's point of view.</li>
<li>Respect: An effective leader should respect others as unique individuals.</li>
<li>Objectivity: No bias or prejudice.</li>
<li>Self Awareness:</li>
<li>Knowing oneself is important.</li>
<li>Be aware of impact you have on others.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-1-4-implications-of-styles-of-leadership-to-practice">2.1.4 Implications of Styles of Leadership to Practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-1-4-implications-of-styles-of-leadership-to-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A nurse manager uses all the styles separately or together depending on his/her flexibility and the circumstances inherent in each situation. There are many similarities and differences in</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="summary">SUMMARY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#summary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this section we learned about leadership and principles of management. A leader was seen as anyone who uses interpersonal skills to influence others to accomplish a goal while leadership is the art of getting others to do what one deems important.</p>
<p>In an attempt to explain leaders and leadership, we looked at two theories.</p>
<p>The trait theory which says leaders have certain physical and emotional characteristics that determine leadership. The situational theory emphasizes on a leader being able to assess, consider its challenges and determine appropriate action. The three leadership styles namely; autocratic, democratic and laissez-faire were discussed.</p>
